- The Complete Guide To Key Burgundy Appellations: Kind: captions Language: en The total number of Appellations in Burgundy is often misinterpreted Although there are around 700 individually named vineyards. The total number of Appellations is 84 according to Frances AOC laws From the basic regional level appellation all the way to the Grand Cru these Appellations guarantee the quality of wine produced in Burgundy 84 Appellations is still a lot to remember However, even though each appellation shows a unique expression of Burgundy's terroir getting familiar with the most prestigious is the best place to start Starting in burgundy is north with the sub region of the cote de nuit. We find the village and appellation of Gevrey-Chambertin Located just twelve kilometers south of Dijon and one of the oldest vineyard sites in Burgundy, Gevrey-Chambertin vineyards date back almost 2,000 years to Gallo Roman times This incredible appellation is home to some of the most sought-after wines in the world all of which are made from Pinot Noir It has nine Grand Cru vineyards more than any other village in Burgundy the suffix in the name Gevrey-Chambertin actually originates from one of these Grand Cru vineyards - The Chambertin A naming trend which was first seen here in 1847, but is now replicated all over Burgundy Gevrey-Chambertin wines are often defined by a deep full-colour Concentrated nose of red berries plum and earthy aromas. They are usually medium to full-bodied with great structure and a long finish Travelling just a few kilometers south in the Cote De Nuit, we reach Chambolle-Musigny a small a very special village Like Gevrey-Chambertin, the town of Chambolle added the name of its most famous vineyard Le Musigny to the villagers name in with a population of less than 500 people this tiny village has around 20 wine producers all of which maintain a close relationship to ensure the prestige of this appellation. A more delicate style of Pinot Noir, Chambolle-Musigny wines are typically a bright ruby colour with red fruit spice and pretty floral aromas The wine has an elegant structure. Thanks to a well-defined minerality and a beautiful long finish Moving to the Cote De Beaune, we find the town and appellation of Beaune and the centre of Burgundy's wine trade with over twenty thousand inhabitants Beaune is a beautiful town of cobbled streets and winding roads with many of Burgundy's largest negociants based here Beaune also boasts the famous hospice de Beaune a 15th century hospital Which is one of the most well preserved examples of French Renaissance architecture It now hosts the most famous wine auction in the world on the third Sunday of each November where many bottles sell for tens of thousands of pounds Although the Cote de Beaune sub region is known primarily for its world-class white wines that Beaune appellation produces primarily red There are no Grand Cru vineyards But there are a number of incredible premier crus which produce a wide variety of beautifully Well-balanced red wines that make a great introduction to red burgundy These wines usually show some pure black fruit and floral aromas Enhanced with spices and earthy notes their medium to full-bodied with red berries and a linear mineral finish So how do Beaune reds differ from other villages? Beaune is an incredible patchwork of different places. It's quite vast You have the North of the village which compares more to the Cotes de Nuits, more masculine We have this vineyard in Beaune Marconnets where we produce some very powerful wine and the last again We have a big structure then if you go further south you have a Beaune Greves. Les Greves means sand So it's a more feminine Precocious Terroir we pick the grapes earlier and the wines are more tender and then you have the Beaune Teurons which is more south Where you have a combination of the two, so it's masculine and feminine at the same time So you have all these different subtle variations, which make it very complex and very intriguing and We have about 35 different premier cru in Beaune. So it gives you the diversity you can make 35 different wines in a circle of Few kilometers around where we here now When it comes to Chardonnay one of the most famous Appellations in the whole of Burgundy is Meursault At the southern end of the Cote de Beaune this large village borders both Puligny and Montrachet Which make the most expensive white wines in the world producing almost a hundred percent Chardonnay Meursault doesn't boast any Grand Cru but lays claim to a few prestigious Premier Cru where this appellation really comes into its own However, is its village wines where you can find incredible value for money Meursault show toasty aromas of nectarine, peach and hazelnut the palate is rich nutty and can have a real Intensity to the fruit. The finish is long with a lovely balanced acidity Heading all the way down to the sub region of the Maconnais, we find another well-known appellation- Pouilly-Fuisse Located in the commune of Fuisse, the wines here at exclusively Chardonnay Pouilly-Fuisse has captured the hearts of many international consumers due to its reputation for producing richer and softer wines than the northern sub-regions of Burgundy As with the whole of the Maconnais sub region, there are no premier or Grand Cru vineyards here However, a recent movement aims to classify around 22% of their vineyards to Premier Cru level The light golden colour of Pouilly-Fuisse wines reflect the warmest southern climate of Burgundy the wine show concentrated citrus and honeycomb aromas and on the palate They have a lovely weighted intensity with baked apples and honey flavors, these wines tend to have a rich but delicate finish these five Appellations are a great introduction to Burgundy with many other names to also look out for Burgundy is the global benchmark for both Chardonnay and Pinot Noir and getting to know these famous names will help you navigate this wonderful region

- Is BURGUNDY WINE Worth the Hype? Visit France's Most Famous Region!: Kind: captions Language: en-US (upbeat music) - Welcome to Burgundy! - [Vince] We have a saying in the wine world, all roads lead to Burgundy, or as you'd say in French, Bourgogne. And well, I'm here! It's the end all be all of wine regions. Pinot Noirs and Chardonnays from Bourgogne are heralded as the best version of these grapes that money can buy. No region is more storied, more studied, or more sought after. And while this area in France has a rich history worthy of exploration, there's also a young generation of winemakers with a keen eye on the future. Bourgogne produces the most famous and expensive wines in existence, but that's only part of the story, and I'm here to uncover the rest. Let's find out why the planet's most famed wine region captivates the hearts and minds of sommeliers, collectors, investors, and normal consumers alike. Welcome to Bourgogne, and welcome to "V is for Vino!" From Paris, it's just a three hour car or train ride to the Bourgogne wine region, which is 140 mile north to south stretch on the east side of France. In the center of Bourgogne is the charming medieval city of Beaune which will be our home base as we explore. - Behind me is the Hospices de Beaune, a historic hospital complex founded in the 1400s but today operates as a museum noted for its Burgundian-Flemish architecture. But more relevant to our conversation, it's home to the annual Hospices de Beaune Wine Auction, the highlight of the year for the Bourgogne region. It's a huge weekend long gala and festival in the fall where the most famous wine makers in the world donate barrels of wine to support various charitable causes. Tourists flood Beaune during this time for a chance to taste the wines, explore the city and sightsee. Things we'll be doing while we're here too. But the true allure of this region lies just outside of Beaune within the vineyards that surround it. It's in these vineyards where we can truly begin to explore the Bourgogne wine industry, which has a history unlike any other. - [Vince] Part of what makes Bourgogne such a prestigious region is the people who shaped it. For Romans recognized its potential over 2000 years ago and planted vines here in the first century AD. During the Middle Ages, vineyards were mostly managed by Cistercian and Benedictine monks who were crucial to the development and documentation of vineyard plots, as well as viticultural and cellar practices. We're gonna talk more about them later. Between the ninth and 15th centuries, during the feudalism of the Middle Ages, large plots of vineyard land began fragmenting amongst noble families and different Climat or vineyard areas started to take shape. Around this time, the wines also began gaining a favorable reputation amongst the lords and ladies of the era, namely the Dukes of Bourgogne and the wines became synonymous with refined tastes and luxury. Entered Napoleon, the emperor of France, in 1804, he introduced the Napoleonic Code, which broke up these feudal estates. You see, before this, these Climat were difficult to buy, sell, or inherit. They were simply given to your eldest son. But after the Napoleonic Code, it was mandated that your vineyard land would be split evenly amongst your heirs. Sounds like a fair idea until 200 years go by and you've got plots that have been fractured so many times that some people end up only owning a few rows of vines. The famous 125 acre vineyard, Clos de Vougeot, for instance, has at times had more than 80 owners, most of whom didn't own a large enough piece of land to produce enough wine to create a business. To solve this, there are entities called cooperatives and negotiants, which pool grapes from multiple vineyard owners so that enough wine can be produced to, you know, have it make sense to sell. - The modern era has seen the wines of Bourgogne reach a level of stratospheric fame that I'm sure those 15th century monks had never even imagined. But none of this happens without one thing in the first place, incredible wine. So welcome to Bourgogne Wine 101 and conveniently, it's a simple course. You ready? For the most part, Bourgogne makes two grapes. Pinot Noir for red and Chardonnay for white. For one of the most complex wine regions in the world, learning its grape varieties is remarkably easy. What makes the region worthy of full on college dissertations on the other hand, is its geography. - [Vince] Bourgogne is small by global standards, especially compared to other regions with similar levels of fame. At 79,000 acres, it's smaller than Napa, Tuscany and only a quarter of the size of its French counterpart Bordeaux. But Bourgogne punches above its weight. Despite producing only 3.4% of AOC wine from France, it generates an astonishing 24% of AOC wine revenue. Much of this is due to its extraordinary soil. 150 to 180 million year old Jurassic period subs soils full of marine limestone, clay and marl to be exact. And what I'd like to say that Bourgogne has a favorable, easygoing wine growing Climate, that would be a lie. In fact, Bourgogne has some of the most challenging weather for producing wine in the world. Because it's landlocked vintages can be inconsistent. Frost and hail isn't uncommon. The cool Climate can make it tough to ripen grapes and humidity can lead to rot on the vines. So how do some of the most transcendent wines on the planet come from here? Well, oenologists have studied this region for so long. They know how to manage this land and mitigate these factors. Frost prevention, hail protection, disease management, and timing the harvest, all in a day's work for a Bourgogne winemaker. - And through this Herculean effort comes some of the world's most pure, intriguing, perplexing, and exalted wines. Because while stressful conditions may make vine growing more difficult, stress on the vines also produces better grapes, similar to how stress on muscles produces bigger biceps. Oh, and by the way, you've probably noticed me calling this region Bourgogne, not Burgundy, the English translation. This is because you'll never see the word Burgundy on a wine label. It's always gonna say, Bourgogne, how it's said in French. Furthermore, for some strange reason, it's the only French wine region we started translating in the first place. We don't translate Bordeaux, Loire or Champagne for example. So to avoid confusion and break bad habits, I'm sticking with Bourgogne. - [Vince] One last tidbit to head off some controversy. The French would actually pronounce this word boor-gohn, or sometimes boor-goin, dropping the last syllable. Instead of saying the English pronunciation, boor-gohn-yuh. This is the equivalent of saying "Par-ee" instead of Paris or "kwah-sahn" instead of croissant, both are correct. It just depends what your foreign language pronunciation philosophy entails. - And now if you excuse me. (upbeat playful music) Okay, I am at the Beaune market and I've got 50 euro. Neither this segment nor Bourgogne for that matter is the place to find it. This is the land of long meals, long wine lists, and even longer books devoted to detailing the region. And while it's not humanly possible for me to fully break down and explain the Bourgogne wine map in the next five minutes, at the very least, this will lay the foundation for you to become an amateur Bourgogne cartographer on your own time. There are five areas in Bourgogne from north to south. They are Chablis and the Grand Auxerrois which is about 50 miles north of the rest of Bourgogne. Then the Côte de Nuits and Côte de Beaune which collectively make what's called the Côte d'Or, Côte Chalonnaise and Mâconnais. Let's start with Chablis and the Grand Auxerrois which makes crisp light mineral driven Chardonnay that isn't even in the same universe as the rich vanilly butter bombs of Northern California. One notable outlier in this area is the AOC St-Bris which is primarily a Sauvignon Blanc growing region. Okay, we'll come back to the Côte d'Or but to the south of it is Côte Chalonnaise which is like the ambitious younger sibling of Bourgogne with something to prove. Great Pinot Noir and Chardonnay wines are made here and they're often more affordable than Côte d'Or wines if slightly leaner and less polished. The five main AOCs here are Rully, Givry, Montagny, Mercurey and Bouzeron. Mercurey is the largest and produces about two thirds of this area's wine and Bouzeron is notable because it is made from Aligoté, a lighter bodied, higher acid white grape. Further south, Mâconnais is known for its Chardonnays, which tend to be fuller than Chablis, but often not as rich or complex as Côte d'Or, but there are some killer wines here at great values comparatively, especially from the AOCs of St-Véran , Viré-Clessé, and most famously, Pouilly-Fuissé. Pouilly-Fuissé wines are riper and a select few producers can indeed rival some of the greatest wines in the region. In the middle is the Côte d'Or, which includes the Côte de Nuit known for its Pinot Noir and Côte de Beaune known for both Chardonnay and Pinot Noir. Before diving into the map of this area, we need to understand why it's so meticulously detailed. From the fifth to 15th century, most vineyard land in Bourgogne was bestowed onto the church from the Dukes of the region. The caretakers of the land, the monks, were very devoted to studying and analyzing it. Their devotion is shown through the copious amount of data they collected in over a thousand years. These very monks are credited with being some of the first to recognize the importance of terroir, studying why two vineyards just a few feet away from each other could produce such different wines. So by the modern era, the monks had already provided guidelines for creating vineyard or Climat boundaries and defining which vineyards had the best potential. But it was the establishment of the formal AOC system in the early 1900s that brought their vision to the masses. - [Vince] The AOC system officially put every Climat into one of four classifications. And while these exist in all five Bourgogne areas, we're only gonna focus on the Côte d'Or for now where they're the most well known. 52% of wine is one of these seven Régionale AOCs, the name of which will appear on the label and are usually the most entry level in terms of quality and price. 37% is village level, which means the grapes are grown around a specific village and usually have a specific village style and are a step up in quality. There are 44 village AOCs, 33 of which are in the Côte d'Or and the name of the village will appear on the label. Some famous ones are Vosne-Romanée, known for elegant Pinots. Puligny-Montrachet for minerally Chardonnays. Meursault for rich Chardonnays. Gevrey-Chambertin for powerful Pinots and Volnay for delicate supple Pinots. Though there are dozens more I don't have time to get into here. Next are the 562 Premier Cru vineyards, which account for 10% of production and are specific Climat or vineyards within villages that have superior terroir. Finally, there are 33 Grand Cru. These AOCs account for just 1% of wine production and are so famous, they'll only list the vineyard name on the label. Names like Chambertin, Clos de Vougeot, Corton-Charlemagne, La Romanée, La Tâche, Montrachet and Romanée-Conti. These are some of the most famous and pricey wines on the planet, and even our sommelier are lucky when we get to try these wines. - The good news is that you don't have to break the bank in order to enjoy Bourgogne. 99% of wines here are not Grand Cru. They're simply great wines for any occasion. Don't believe me? Behind me is the village of Pernand-Vergeles. And from here you can actually see all four types of Climat. Reégionale, Pernand-Vergeles village, Pernand-Vergeles Premier Cru and Grand Cru Corton. All tucked nicely next to each other. And just because Corton may be out my reach, a nice regional Côte de Beaune Chardonnay with lunch or a village level Pernand-Vergelesses Pinot Noir for dinner is not. If you switch your mentality from tough to understand to fun to explore, you'll see why Bourgogne is the place for wine and knowledge lovers. (light airy upbeat music) - [Vince] Chablis part of Bourgogne, yes, but an anomaly. It's 60 miles north of the rest of the region and very cold. It's actually closer to the chili hills of Champagne than Bourgogne. Now you may ask why bother making wine in such chili conditions? The answer is in the esteemed Kimmeridgian soil. This soil contains 150 million year old fossilized marine and oyster shelves from back when this whole part of the earth was underwater. Today, it produces some of the greatest Chardonnays on the planet, albeit a much different style of Chardonnay than you may be used to. Third generation wine maker Eve Grossot, who works with her father at the winery tells me more. - Now Chablis is really interesting because it's way up north. If there's one word for Chablis, it's mineral driven wines because we study this. And while you're there, you can join Vino VIP and visit our members only section with a ton of behind the scenes videos and bonus content. Just thought I'd let you know. - There is no sentence in the world that breaks a sommelier's heart more than I don't like Chardonnay. Customers said it to me all the time, but I'm going on record now to say something in response that I never have before, you're wrong. It isn't Chardonnay the grape you don't like. You have a problem with the style of Chardonnay you've tried. You see, Chardonnay is like the tofu of the wine world. It can be anything and is very influenced by the wine maker who creates it. Chardonnay is actually the most widely planted white grape in the world and it's Bourgogne that made it so famous and in every style imaginable. Sparkling wine, check. Light, crisp with screaming acidity, citrus, and salinity from Chablis, check. Medium bodied with light oak, balanced acidity and rich yellow orchard fruits like pear, peach, and apple from Côte Chalonnaise or Mâconnais, check. Full bodied, opulent and oaked with creamy vanilla, toast, minerality and tropical fruits from the Côte d'Or, check. And everything in between, including age worthy wines that turn honeyed, waxy, mushroomy and complex. While you can't neatly summarize Chardonnay from Bourgogne, you can start to learn which areas make the styles that you enjoy. If Chardonnay is like tofu, Pinot Noir is like single origin chocolate or coffee. It absorbs the terroir it comes from more than any other grape in the world and it's up to the farmer to baby it ever so carefully through the harvest so it can show off the best version of itself. But the results are worth the effort. The Pinot Noirs from Bourgogne are undebatably extraordinary. A quick refresher on Pinot, it's light to medium in body, high in acidity, usually fairly low tannin and the fruit character lives in bright red to dark red, cherry, pomegranate, raspberry, black cherry and strawberry. But just like how putting Chardonnay in a box is hard to do here, the same can be said about Pinot Noir. Each Climat has a distinct style and I'll be honest, there's no flavor that you can't find in Bourgogne Pinot. You can encounter blue and black fruits. How about herbs and spices like thyme, pepper, or cinnamon, sure. Flowers like violets or roses, yep. Coffee, mocha, salami, tobacco, licorice, orange peel. If it exists, you can probably find it. But the one thing, almost every Pinot Noir here has, a primal earthiness, soil, moss, mushroom, truffles, and yes, sometimes funk in the best possible way. Bourgogne doesn't have chateaus like other French wine regions, they have Domaines and it's a small but important distinction. A chateau is more of what you may think of as a traditional wine estate. A place where there's a wine making facility, sometimes a grand castle like structure surrounded by vineyards that are harvested for use by that winery. But a Domaine is different. A Domaine is a winery that usually owns vineyards from many appellations across a wine region. For example, a Domaine may own 10 vineyards across 10 different appellations and make 10 different Chardonnays from those vineyards. And it's why Bourgogne labels can be confusing to newcomers. Two labels that look exactly the same with only a few words changed can be hundreds of dollars apart in price. And on the flip side, sometimes two different producers can make wine from the same exact grapes. So the label may look different, but the only change inside the bottle is who vinified the wine. So let's break down how to read these labels. - [Vince] At the end of the day, what we wanna determine is where the wine comes from and who made it. Let's start with where it comes from. Vin de Bourgogne will be on most wines, and this just tells you the wine is from Bourgogne. If you don't know any other words on the label, this is how you determine the wine isn't a Bordeaux or Rhône wine, for instance. It also tells you that it's more than likely a Pinot Noir if red or Chardonnay if white, because that's what they make here. Next we need to determine where it is on our hierarchy pyramid. If it's Régionale, it'll simply have the region name like Bourgogne. If it's Village level, it will have the village name listed. In both these cases, the official AOC designation will match the bigger text on the labels. If it's a Premier Cru, the wine will still list the village name, both on the label and in the AOCs but will add the Premier Cru designation and usually tell you the specific Premier Cru vineyard the grapes came from. Finally, Grand Cru wines will only list the vineyard name in big, bold letters and the AOC will match as every grand crew in Bourgogne is its own AOC. But what about who makes it? The producer name is often listed in smaller letters up top or below the location information, though there's no hard and fast rules here. Some producers hide their name in tiny texts. Some like big, bold branding. Finally, there will sometimes be other information like if the vineyard is a monopole where the grapes were vinified and if the vineyard is a clos or wall vineyard and other details. But honestly, I wouldn't worry about them as you start out. The most important info is knowing where the grapes come from and who did the vinification. - The more Bourgogne wines you try, the better you'll get at understanding the labels and knowing your favorite places and producers. And because this region focuses on single Climat and varietals for their wines, you get purity of fruit. There's no blending of other grapes or vineyard plots. If Bordeaux is like blended scotch, Bourgogne is single malt. These wines are a true expression of the grapes, terroir and the year they come from. I did too, it was my first time here and it's a humbler region than I imagined in my head. There are far fewer luxury hotels, grand tasting rooms and touristy restaurants than I thought there would be in the most renowned wine region on the planet. Instead, I found some form of the following everywhere I went. Adrien and his father opening some special vintages for the crew and I. Simon and Bastien discussing their dinner plans and France going right back to work in the vineyard the second filming was over. Family wineries, quaint villages, homey food and winemakers focused on making and drinking wine above all else. (light uplifting upbeat music) The Roche de Solutré, a giant rock formation breaking from the earth as if to be reaching for the sky, formed from 150 million year old limestone and thousands of years of erosion and tectonic activity. It's a striking presence in the landscape of southern Bourgogne. People who have a passion for wine often see Bourgogne as the quintessential wine region. Its famed history and ancient geology laid a solid foundation for its success today and like layers in the rock, previous generations know-how and wisdom have been passed down to its descendants slowly building this region up one layer at a time. Today's winemakers know they'll have to do the same, preserve the land for the future and act as stewards of this knowledge for their children as well. Because of this, wine lovers like me across the globe have a never ending mountain of exploration to try and ascend. When it comes to Bourgogne, no matter how high you climb, you'll never know at all, never reach the top. But what you do know for certain is that you can come back here again and again and always find something new, something genuine. Winemakers aren't concerned with anything other than the purity of their product. They care about honesty, representing a moment in a bottle. This makes every new trek into Bourgogne predictably unpredictable. Every new ascent, perfectly imperfect. Bourgogne is full of adventure for those willing to seek it. I hope you enjoyed this episode and we'll see you next time on "V is for Vino." (gentle upbeat music) Hey, Vince here, hope you enjoyed the episode. If you have a moment, follow us on Instagram. And if you really wanna support, please consider joining Vino VIP on visforvino.com. It's our members-only club with a ton of benefits. Thanks for watching and see you soon! These monks created a style of Chardonnay that took its name from Burgundy's northern region - Chablis. In the 16th and 17th centuries, Chablis had a virtual monopoly on the lucrative Parisian market. Around this time, the English started to import substantial quantities. River ways and canals were used as an effective means of transportation and this really allowed Chablis to become one of the most extensively-planted vineyards in the world. Chablis is the most northerly growing region in Burgundy and grows Chardonnay exclusively with seven grand cru and 40 premier cru vineyards. The secret to this region's style lies in an ancient seabed of fossilized shells called Kimmeridgian soil, which infuses the minerality of the limestone into the wine. For me, the wines of Chablis are the purest expression of the Chardonnay grape. The use of stainless steel tanks and limited oak influences means that the wine retains its acidity, minerality, and green apple notes. Pouilly-Fuissé is the most well-known appellation of the region, and two thirds of its production is shipped to North America. needed wine for mass and for their own entertainment monks could really focus on improving quality because they had time they had sellers they were able to keep records and they were pretty well educated for those times the first important monastery in burgundy were the benedictines of cuny who received for example the vineyard that is today known as romani conti in 1232 the other really important monastery were the cistercians who were founded a bit later than the benedictines near nuisance george and were the first ones to make chablis and the first owners of the vineyard known as claude boujou the monks were real wine nerds and they are the reason why burgundy today is so well understood but nerds also make things more complicated than they should be and this is the reason why the vineyards of burgundy are today so difficult to understand as they have been broken up into smaller and smaller parcels in order to isolate different characteristics and different quality levels another really important character in the history of burgundy was the duke of burgundy philip the bolt who wanted to decrease yields and increase quality in the region he ordered vintners to rip out garment vines and plant pinot noir instead in 1395. he actually said that gummy is harmful to human beings which is a bit rough but without him burgundy might not have been recognized as one of the greatest wine growing regions in the world burgundy as a whole includes 30 000 hectares of vineyards and the corridor is right in the middle of the region and it is known for producing really high quality wines the cote d'or is only roughly 50 kilometers long and it sits between dijon and santine the name codor either means golden slope or it means could dorian as the exposition of the slopes is towards the east the orient cordor is also the name of the department the administrative district the code door sits right on the fault line between the mauvon hills and the zone plane this part of france was under water during the jurassic period and when the water receded crustaceans and shells died fell to the ground and limestone formed from their remains over time this is why most parts of burgundy contain high levels of limestones and most vineyards are made up of clay and limestone this little stone is actually a piece of limestone from a vineyard called romani conti it doesn't taste that much though in general the best quality grapes come from the middle part of the slope the bottom part of the slope is most of the times too fertile for high quality wine production and the top part of the slope is oftentimes too sparse so that wines tend to be more light and not as profound and concentrated in this middle section of the vineyard which tends to be at an elevation of 250 to 300 meters above sea level you can find most of the premier crew and grand cru sites at the top of the slopes you can find mostly village level wines and at the bottom of the slopes you also find the large level wines or regional wines but i'm going to talk about the quality levels in a minute the coat door is split up into two parts you have the kote nui in the north which is known for amazing red wines and you have the kotu bone in the south which is known for great red wines and great white wines in the courty you find those famous villages called jerry shambatan morissandinis and von of people in this classification vineyards are classified not producers like it is the case in bordeaux and not individual wines as it is the case with most wine critics today so at the bottom you have the regional appellations like bogor and rush that represents 52 percent of the region above that you have the village that are named after the village such as von roman village this includes 37 percent of all wines in burgundy above that you have the premier queer appellations with 640 different sites like bon romanese premier cru this makes up only 10 percent of the total production and then you have the best of the best the concrete like for example rich poor there are only 33 grand cruise in burgundy that account for around one percent of the total production easy isn't it so the climate in burgundy is continental with cold winters and relatively warm summers the region often has problems with spring frost you might have seen those beautiful pictures of vineyards with lots of different fires burning between the vines this looks very nice but spring frost is a real issue and can cost the wine makers a lot of money if it happens burgundy also has problems with regular hail storms or rain falling during spring or during harvest this is why vintage variation in burgundy is a real factor when you buy wine when it comes to grape varieties there are really two grape varieties that rule one is pinot noir as a red grape variety and the other one is chardonnay as a white grape variety these two grape varieties are now planted all around the world but producers still look to burgundy for inspiration when it comes to making great pinot noir and chardonnay people often forget that there are also other grape varieties being grown in burgundy for example ali gotti gamay and sauvignon blanc in burgundy itself grape varieties rarely appear on the label as vineyards and producer names are normally used in order to distinguish different wines but now let's talk about viticulture and wine making in the corridor wineries overall are pretty small in burgundy the average domain only has 6.5 hectares of vineyards the vine density is super high in burgundy 10 000 vines per hectare so one vine per square meter is pretty normal in the region this does normally increase quality as the vines have to struggle more in order to get nutrients from the soil but it also means that most vineyards are not mechanized because you can't really drive a tractor through those very densely planted vineyards the overall yields in burgundy are pretty low less than 60 hectare liters per hectare even less for premier crew and grand cru sites wine making in the cote d'or varies from producer to producer but pretty much all of the great producers use barik's small wooden barrels for their red wines and their white wines but there are differences when it comes to de-stemming maceration time use of cultured yeast and filtration for example especially the topic of - Red Winemaking In Burgundy Explained: Kind: captions Language: en Wine making. The practice of fermenting grape juice and turning it into wine has been around for thousands of years But there are many nuances to this process and often different regions will have their own specific processes and methods Wine making has become an intricate and deeply personal process Wine making practices in Burgundy are partially protected by appellation laws, though Wine makers still have a large amount of control over their final decisions For example in Burgundy the only two permitted grapes for making red wine at Pinot Noir and Gamay With the latter only being used in the southern sub-regions The wine making process begins with harvesting when the grapes have reached their optimal ripeness and balance between sugar and acidity levels In Burgundy many of the top producers will harvest grapes by hand However machine harvesting is also allowed for all wines, except Grand Cru The harvested grapes will then go through a rigorous sorting process to make sure that only the best berries are sent for production This sorting can either be done by hand or with a high-tech machine that removes unwanted berries Once the grapes have been sorted they begin the process of vinification fermenting grape juice to make wine Firstly, grapes will be put into a vessel such as a stainless steel vat, cement tank or old oak cask Traditionally grapes will be crushed before entering the vats to release their juice however, many producers nowadays prefer to keep their grapes unbruised and uncrushed putting them into the vat as whole bunches including the stems This can change the structure and finesse of the tannins in the wine once in the vat, the red grape juice along with grape skins, seeds and possibly stems will start the process of maceration which Is the extraction of colour, flavour and tannins. During maceration, alcoholic fermentation occurs either naturally or through the addition of selective yeasts to the vat. Alcoholic fermentation is the process of yeast consuming the grapes natural sugars. There are three natural byproducts of fermentation: ethanol, carbon dioxide and heat. Whilst in the vat, Wine makers may choose to increase the extraction of colour and tannins by either pumping over the must, which is the fermenting juice, Or cap punching. Pushing the grape skins down into the liquid This cap punching method known as Pigeage and French was traditionally done by a wine maker walking and stomping down on the grapes. After alcoholic fermentation The existing juice is removed from the skins and the skins go through a pressing process to release any additional juice. In Burgundy many producers use a bladder press which is a very gentle process where grapes go into a tube with a bladder. The bladder is filled with air, crushing the grapes and releasing any remaining juice The wine will then be transferred into oak barrels It's very easy to understand the fermenting process, when the grapes arrive We press we extract the juice, the must. In the must we have a natural yeast and the yeast eats the sugar and transform the sugar in alcohol It's very simple. Does the fermentation process stop naturally or do you have to stop it? This fermentation stops naturally because when the yeast don't have sugar She dead and the fermentation stop naturally. Once in the oak barrels the process of malolactic fermentation occurs. During malolactic fermentation Microorganisms naturally found in grape juice Transformed tart malic acid found in green apples into soft lactic acid, which is found in milk This process softens the wine and helps to give the wine a smoother texture The wine is then left in oak barrels for maturation over a number of months during what is known as elevage. This step is critical to the development of flavors and aromas in a red wine. In Burgundy, French oak barrels of 228 litres act as the perfect vessel for maturing the wine Tiny pores in the would allow for micro oxygenation softening the wine and creating a smoother final product So what are the different steps for fermenting a red wine? If you want red wine particularly with the Pinot Noir you need colour and you don't have any colour in the juice You need the skin. For the white wine, you press before the malolactic fermentation And for the red wine you press after. The grapes goes into big vats in native oak But you do some different things during the fermentation for the red the pumping over we do some pigeage and When the alcoholic fermentation is completely finished you press and after the wine go to big barrels The oak barrels used for maturation may be reused three to four times with newer oak having a larger impact on a wines flavour on average wine makers will use more new oak for higher quality wines with a more complex and structured wine able to hold up to the intensity of the new oak. In Burgundy's Cote D'Or the richer red wines of the Cote de Nuit, tend to have more oak than in the Cote de Beaune Maturation and these oak barrels for the best red Burgundy's may last fifteen to twenty four months After the maturation phase the wine is transferred out of the oak barrels and back into a stainless steel vat During what is known as racking and blending Wines that are made up of different vineyard plots will all be blended together into the tank The wine is often still cloudy at this point. So a process of fining may be done to remove the unwanted particles Thus clarifying the liquid Fining agents may be natural proteins such as those found in egg whites or fish though Synthetic products can also be used most commonly in vegan wines the final step of the process is Filtration and bottling with the wines being transferred from tank to bottle and the final cork being added Bottles are then labeled and ready for sale The whole process of red wine making in Burgundy can take anywhere from a few months to well over two years During this time Wine makers need to make dozens, If not hundreds of decisions along the way to ensure that they end up with the best possible final product This is a simplistic guideline to the production of the great red wines of Burgundy as Each and every wine maker will adopt and employ their own techniques to create their own personal style

  1. @stevenholt4936 [10 likes]: Good tips, John. I would add this: find a wine merchant whose buyers you trust - I buy almost exclusively from the Wine Society these days, having used many over the years.
I agree about off vintages: one of the most disappointing experiences for me was tasting Lafite from an off year. However, more fruit forward years can be very useful. For example, I am drinking 2014 clarets at the moment (a Beaumont this weekend) whilst waiting for the '10s, '15s, '16s, '18s and '19s to mature.
Over-buying is so tempting. I have stopped buying en primeur (I am 68) and so my modest collection of 700 or so should diminish gracefully along with its owner over time. Mind you, my mum is over 100 and still going, so perhaps I will come to regret this decision. 
I record all my purchases on a simple Exel spreadsheet, along with its purchase price, drinking window, remaining stock, who I drank each bottle with, with what foods and my comments. I find that is sufficient for my needs.
Also, I agree about buying smaller quantities - my recent purchases have all been in either threes or sixes. I do like to check on the progress of particular wines over time, so for Bordeaux, for example, I stick to half cases usually. 
Lastly, as we rarely get Texas weather here in the UK, that point is less of an issue, although I would say that proper storage is still very important; I keep my wines ready for drinking in a chiller cabinet at home and the rest in the merchant's cellars.

decanting two do you need to decant all your wines number three what is the best shape for a wine decanter and number four we'll look at several types of decanters what is decanting decanting is centuries old the romans let their decanted wine sit out for hours believing it brought out the wines aromas and mellowed its harsh tannins they were actually spot on that's pretty much what it does a decanting wine is essentially the process of pouring the contents from the bottle into another vessel a decanter typically the wine is then served from the decanter now generally speaking the canning serves two purposes number one it separates the wine from sediment that may have formed in the bottle and number two it gets air or oxygen to the wine so its aromas and flavors will be more vibrant when it's served the canning accelerates the breathing process which increases the wines aromas and flavors that it gets from both the grapes and oak barrels it also softens the taste of the tannins that cause harshness and astringency in young red wines now to understand a little bit more about what i mean by breathing or the breathing process check out my video letting your wine breathe number two do you decant all wines the simplest rule of thumb is to cat wines that are red old and bold to be fair there really aren't many wines that get worse with the canning as far as white wines are concerned when white wines are vinified or produced in contact with their skins then decanting may improve them the same way it improves many red wines any skin contact wines will benefit the canning softens their tannins also to warm up a white wine coming straight out of the fridge pour it into a room temperature decanter now when it comes to wines in general the rule of thumb that i mentioned earlier is the way to go both old and bold wines will benefit from decanting but for different reasons the reason for decanting old wine is to remove the juice from the sediment that has collected in the bottom over time this is a good idea for reds five or six years old or older to decant these aged reds you've been storing it on its side so what you want to do is you want to stand the bottle straight up for several hours or even a day before consumption this lets the sediment fall to the bottom when pouring your wine pour your wine slowly into the decanter tilt the bottle and stop pouring when you see the sediment or cloudiness reach the shoulder or the neck the sediment will get settled right here these bottles are designed to do this now to understand more about this you might want to check out my video wine bottles the big three bordeaux burgundy and alsace now don't try to pour past the sediment you'll end up with a bunch of sediment in your glass and that's not so good with these older wines older than five or six years old once the juice is removed from the sediment the decanting process is over and the wine is ready to drink now now listen to this this is really important don't leave your old wines out to breathe this will shorten its consumption window now for big bold young reds the purpose of the canning is different it's to introduce oxygen to the wine when it comes in contact with oxygen the aromas and flavors become stronger making it easier to assess and enjoy when you decant young bold reds pour the wine quickly let the wine get plenty of air mixed into it like this you see how quickly i'm doing this you're letting a lot of oxygen get in oxygen will cause tannins to soften up making an aggressive wine much easier to drink after an hour or so with the air i really recommend that you decant most young reds especially bold varieties like cabs syrah and nibiolos now some wines can be stored overnight in your decanter but for older wines if you don't finish them you really need to return them to the bottle now here's another rule of thumb regarding decanting your wines if you start off with a good wine the canning it takes it too very good the canning and letting it sit or rest takes it to great and decanting it and letting it open up for an hour or so maximizes the flavors and your experience how are you doing there are you more effectively they're definitely easier to clean than thin neck versions wide neck decanters are the most popular and will work well with most wine drinkers the size of the opening will obviously - Billionaire Fires Maid for Being Too Educated — Her PhD Thesis Topic Will Shock You: Kind: captions Language: en The marble floors of the Witmore estate gleamed under the morning light, reflecting a world of wealth that seemed to exist in a different dimension from the one where most people lived. Elena Vasquez moved through the grand hallway with practiced silence, her cleaning cartwheels barely making a whisper against the polished stone. She had learned long ago that invisibility was the greatest skill a domestic worker could possess in homes like these. The less you were noticed, the longer you kept your job. At 42 years old, Elena carried herself with a dignity that no amount of scrubbing or dusting could diminish. Before we jump back in, tell us where you're tuning in from. Her hands, though callous from years of labor, moved with a peculiar precision that suggested they had once been trained for something else entirely. Her dark eyes, often downcast in the presence of her employers, held depths of intelligence that she had learned to carefully conceal behind a mask of pleasant servitude. The Witmore mansion sprawled across 15 acres in the hills overlooking San Francisco, a testament to the fortune that Theodore Witmore had amassed through his tech empire. Whitmore Digital Solutions had revolutionized cloud computing infrastructure, making Theodore one of the youngest billionaires in Silicon Valley history. At 53, he ruled his business empire with an iron fist and expected the same unquestioning obedience in his personal domain. Elena had worked in the Whitmore household for nearly 3 years, one of a rotating staff of 12 who maintained the property. She arrived each morning at 6:00, worked until 3:00 in the afternoon, and returned to her small studio apartment in the Mission District where she lived alone. Her co-workers knew little about her beyond the fact that she was reliable, thorough, and never caused problems. She rarely joined them for lunch, preferring to eat alone in the staff room with a book that she always kept carefully hidden in her bag. On this particular morning, Elena was assigned to clean Theodore's private study, a task that was usually reserved for the head housekeeper, but had fallen to her due to a staffing shortage. She pushed her cart through the double doors, taking in a vast room lined with leatherbound books that she suspected had never been opened. Theodore collected rare first editions the way some men collected cars, more for status than genuine appreciation. The desk dominated the center of the room, an imposing structure of mahogany that probably cost more than Elena made in a year. Papers were scattered across its surface in carefully organized chaos, financial reports, and business proposals that represented decisions affecting thousands of employees. Elena began her work methodically, dusting the bookshelves first, working her way around the room's perimeter before approaching the more personal spaces. As she worked, her eyes inevitably drifted to the spines of the books. philosophy, economics, political theory, volumes on leadership and innovation. Her fingers paused on a copy of Marcus Aurelius's meditations, a particularly beautiful edition bound in burgundy leather. She couldn't help herself. Her hand reached out, pulling the book gently from the shelf. The pages fell open to a passage she had memorized years ago, words that had sustained her through darker times. She was so absorbed that she didn't hear the door open behind her. What exactly do you think you're doing? The voice was cold, sharp as a blade. Elena's heart lurched as she spun around, the book still in her hands. Theodore Whitmore stood in the doorway, his tall frame filling the space with an authority that needed no announcement. "You were reading." "Those books are valuable collector's items, not entertainment for the help." Elena felt her face flush with shame and something else, something that burned hotter than embarrassment. It was the familiar sting of being reminded of her place, of being reduced to nothing more than her current circumstances. She forced herself to look down, to play the role she had perfected over the years. "Of course, sir, it won't happen again." Theodore moved to his desk, his attention already shifting away from her as if she were merely an inconvenient interruption to his morning. "See that it doesn't. I expect professionalism from my staff, not curiosity about things that don't concern them. Elena returned to her work, her movements now stiff with tension. She could feel Theodore's presence in the room like a physical weight, monitoring her every action. She dusted the desk carefully, avoiding the papers, focusing on the edges and corners. That's when she saw it. Partially hidden beneath a quarterly earnings report was a letter on official university letterhead. The header read Stanford University Department of Sociology. But years of academic training had ingrained certain reflexes. Research was in her blood. Even now, even here, even while wearing a housekeeper's uniform and pretending to be invisible. Is there a problem? Theodore's voice cut through her thoughts. No, sir. Just being thorough. Thoroughess doesn't require staring at my private correspondence. Elena's jaw tightened. Elena gathered her supplies quickly, eager to escape the suffocating atmosphere of the room. As she reached the door, Theodore spoke again. "And Elena, stay curious about your work, not mine." She nodded without turning around and left, her heart pounding in a way that had nothing to do with fear and everything to do with a rising frustration she had been suppressing for far too long. The rest of the day passed in a blur of routine tasks. Elena cleaned bathrooms, vacuumed hallways, changed linens, and polished silverware. She worked alongside Carmen, a cheerful woman from Guatemala who had been with the Whites for 5 years, and James, a quiet man in his 60s who had worked in various wealthy households for most of his life. During their brief lunch break, Carmen noticed Elena's unusual silence. You okay, Mija? You seem tense today. Elena forced a smile. Just tired. Didn't sleep well. That study assignment getting to you? I hate that room. So many books, so much dusting. Mr. Whitmore acts like they're made of gold. Carmen laughed, but there was an edge to it. You're so cynical, James. Not cynical, realistic. I've worked in houses like this for 30 years. The faces change, the addresses change, but the attitude never does. We're furniture to them. Useful furniture, but furniture nonetheless. Elena wanted to argue to say that surely some wealthy people saw their staff as human beings, but the words died in her throat. Her encounter with Theodore that morning had reinforced a truth she had been living with for years. In these houses, in this world, she was defined entirely by her function. Her thoughts, her education, her entire past life, none of it mattered. She was a cleaner. That was her identity in their eyes. And anything that suggested otherwise was seen as threatening. The evening arrived with Elena bone tired but unable to rest. She sat in her small apartment, surrounded by the only possessions that really mattered to her. Books lined makeshift shelves constructed from planks and cinder blocks. Her laptop, old but functional, sat on a folding table that served as her desk. Preparing and narrating this story took us a lot of time. So, if you are enjoying it, subscribe to our channel. It means a lot to us. Now, back to the story. and in a cardboard box under her bed, carefully protected in plastic sleeves, were the remnants of another life. She pulled the box out and opened it slowly as if handling something sacred. Inside were diplomas, transcripts, letters of recommendation, and a thick bound manuscript that represented seven years of her life. The title page read, "Invisible labor, the exploitation and erasure of domestic workers in contemporary American society." below it, her name, as it had once been recognized, Dr. Elena Vasquez, PhD in sociology, Columbia University. She traced her fingers over the embossed letters, remembering the woman who had written those words. That Elena had been confident, passionate, determined to change the world through research and advocacy. She had conducted hundreds of interviews with domestic workers across the country, documenting their experiences, analyzing patterns of exploitation and proposing policy reforms. Her dissertation committee had called it groundbreaking. One professor had told her it would make her career, but life had other plans. The academic job market had been brutal. Despite her credentials, despite the praise for her work, the positions she had applied for went to candidates with better connections, with research that was less controversial, less politically charged. Your work is important, she had been told repeatedly, but perhaps too focused on advocacy rather than pure scholarship. Translation: You make people uncomfortable. Then came the personal disasters, her mother's cancer diagnosis, the mounting medical bills, the decision to leave New York and move back to California to care for her, the savings that evaporated, the desperate search for any work that could pay rent and medical expenses, the PhD that became a liability in job interviews because she was overqualified for everything except the academic positions that didn't want her. Domestic work had been a last resort, a way to stay afloat while she figured out her next move. But days became weeks, weeks became months, and months became years. Her mother had passed away 8 months ago, leaving Elena alone with grief and the suffocating realization that she had lost herself somewhere along the way. The woman who had fought so hard to give voice to invisible workers had become invisible herself. She opened her laptop and navigated to a folder she visited every few weeks. Inside were drafts of articles she had written but never submitted, research proposals for projects she would never conduct, and most recently a grant application for a community advocacy center she dreamed of creating. It would offer legal assistance, education programs, and support services for domestic workers. It would be everything her research had shown was desperately needed. But dreams required money, and money required either wealthy benefactors or institutional support, neither of which were available to a woman cleaning houses in San Francisco. She had filled out the grant application anyway, knowing it was feudal, knowing that organizations funded projects from established academics and nonprofit leaders, not from housekeepers with outdated CVs and gaps in their professional history. Elena closed the laptop and leaned back in her chair, staring at the water stained ceiling of her apartment. The building was old, the walls thin enough that she could hear her neighbors television through the wall. This was her reality now. This was what happened when you tried to fight systems too big to notice your existence. A knock on her door startled her from her thoughts. She wasn't expecting anyone. Opening the door cautiously, she found Carmen standing in the hallway holding a casserole dish. "I made too much," Carmen said with a knowing smile that suggested this was a polite fiction. "Thought you might be hungry." Elena felt a wave of gratitude that threatened to break through her carefully maintained composure. "That's very kind of you. Please come in." Carmen entered, glancing around the tiny apartment with approval rather than pity. You keep it nice. I like all the books. You read a lot. It's a habit I can't break, Elena said, accepting the casserole. Can I get you some tea? They sat together in Elena's small space, sharing tea in conversation. Carmen talked about her children, three kids she was putting through school with money earned scrubbing other people's toilets and washing other people's dishes. She talked about her dreams for them. How education was going to be their ticket to a different life. What about you? Carmen asked. You have family. Not anymore. My mother passed last year. I'm sorry, Mija. That's hard. What did you do before the cleaning work? Elena hesitated. She had become skilled at deflecting these questions, at keeping her past safely locked away. But something about Carmen's open, genuine interest made her want to be honest, even if just for a moment. I was a researcher. I studied sociology, worked with communities, tried to understand how systems of power affect everyday people. Carmen's eyes widened like a scholar with a degree and everything, something like that. So, why are you cleaning houses? It was the question Elena had asked herself a thousand times. Because life happens. Because degrees don't pay medical bills. Because the world doesn't always work the way we hope it will. Carmen nodded slowly, her expression thoughtful. You know what my grandmother used to say? She said, "The world breaks everyone eventually, but some people become strong in the broken places." Maybe that's you, Elena. Maybe you're becoming strong in the broken places. After Carmen left, Elena sat alone with those words echoing in her mind. Strong in the broken places. It was a nice thought, poetic even. But she didn't feel strong. She felt tired. She felt like a fraud every time she put on her uniform and pretended that her thoughts didn't extend beyond dusting and mopping. She felt like she was suffocating under the weight of her own unused potential. She returned to the box under her bed and pulled out her dissertation again, seven years of research, hundreds of interviews, thousands of hours of analysis, all of it documenting a reality that the world seemed determined to ignore. She had written about women like Carmen, like James, like herself now. She had written about the invisible architecture of American comfort, built on the backs of people who cleaned and cooked and cared, all while remaining unseen and unvalued. One section of her dissertation had focused specifically on how employers perceived their domestic staff. She had documented the cognitive dissonance that allowed wealthy families to claim they valued their workers while simultaneously denying them basic dignities. The workers were described as part of the family in the same breath that they were forbidden from using the front door or eating from the same dishes as their employers. She had written about the performative nature of domestic work, how workers had to constantly manage their employers comfort by appearing neither too intelligent nor too ignorant, neither too friendly nor too distant. They had to read moods, anticipate needs, and above all remain non-threatening to the egos of those they served. Reading her own words now, living the reality she had once studied from an academic distance felt surreal. She had become her own research subject. The irony was not lost on her. The next morning, Elena arrived at the Whitmore estate at her usual time. The fog was thick coming off the bay, wrapping the mansion in a gray cocoon that made it seem even more isolated from the world beyond its gates. She changed into her uniform in the staff room, pinning her hair back and checking her reflection to ensure she looked appropriately invisible. Mrs. Chen, the head housekeeper, was waiting with the day's assignments. She was a stern woman in her late 50s, originally from Hong Kong, who ran the household staff with military efficiency. Elena, you'll be working the East Wing today. Guest rooms need full service. The Whites are hosting a dinner party tomorrow night and everything must be perfect. Of course, Mrs. Chin and Elena. Mr. Whitmore mentioned you were in his study yesterday. He's particular about that space. No one goes in there without clearance from now on. Understood. Yes, ma'am. Elena spent the morning working through the guest rooms, each one larger than her entire apartment. She changed linens, dusted antique furniture, cleaned bathrooms that sparkled like jewelry stores. The monotony of the work allowed her mind to wander, a dangerous habit she had tried to break but never quite managed. She thought about the letter she had seen on Theodore's desk. A Stanford research proposal about labor exploitation. The irony was thick enough to choke on. Here was a man who benefited directly from the exploitation of workers, funding academic research about that very exploitation. It was the kind of contradiction that would have made excellent material for a paper if she were still in a position to write such things. Around midday, Elena was dusting a hallway when she heard voices coming from the conservatory, a glass enclosed garden room that Mrs. Whitmore used for her morning tea. Elena slowed her movements, not intentionally eavesdropping, but not exactly trying to avoid it either. The university is being ridiculous about this, Theodore's voice carried clearly. They want me to fund research that essentially criticizes people like me. Do they not see the irony? Well, that's academia for you, dear. This was Vivian Whitmore, Theodore's wife. Her voice carrying the cultivated smoothness of old money in private schools. They bite the hand that feeds them and call it intellectual integrity. The proposal is about domestic labor exploitation. They want to interview household staff, document working conditions, publish findings that will inevitably paint employers in a negative light and they want me to pay for it. Viven laughed, a sound like crystal tinkling. So tell them no. Fund something else. God knows there are enough researchers desperate for money. Find one studying something less inconvenient. It's not that simple. The university president specifically requested this. Apparently, the researcher is some rising star in their sociology department. Turning it down would cause political problems I don't need right now. Elena's heart was pounding. She should move away. Should make her presence known before she heard more. But she was frozen, caught between professional self-preservation and burning curiosity. Then fund it and ignore the results, Vivien said dismissively. Whatever they find, it's not like anyone who matters will care. These academic studies come out all the time, and nothing ever changes. The world needs people to clean houses and serve meals. That's just how things work. True enough. I'll have my assistant draft a response. Keep them happy. Maintain the relationship with Stanford. It's all just theater anyway. Elena heard movement and quickly pushed her cart down the hall, her pulse racing. She found an empty bedroom and closed the door behind her, leaning against it as she tried to steady her breathing. She felt sick, not just because of the casual dismissal she had just heard, but because of the deeper truth it revealed. Nothing ever changes. That's what Vivien had said. And she was right. Elena had spent years researching and documenting exploitation. Had poured her heart into work that she believed could make a difference. And for what? So people like Theodore Whitmore could fund research they had no intention of taking seriously. So wealthy families could point to studies and say, "Yes, isn't that terrible?" before going back to treating their staff like sophisticated appliances. She thought about her dissertation sitting in a box under her bed. She thought about the grant proposal she had written for a community center that would never exist. She thought about all the women she had interviewed who had shared their stories with the hope that someone somewhere might care enough to change things. The anger that rose in her chest was hot and bitter. It was the anger of years of diminishment, of watching her education and expertise become liabilities rather than assets. It was the anger of being told that her work was too political, too controversial, too uncomfortable when all she had done was document reality. It was the anger of being reduced to a uniform and a mop. While the very people who benefited from her invisibility funded research about invisibility. A knock on the door made her jump. Elena, you in there? It was Carmen's voice. Elena opened the door quickly composing her face into neutrality. Yes, sorry. Just taking a quick break. Carmen studied her with concern. You okay? You look upset. I'm fine. Just feeling a bit off today. Mrs. Chin is looking for you. Once everyone in the staff room for a meeting, the entire household staff gathered in the cramped room off the kitchen. Mrs. Chin stood at the front, her expression even more severe than usual. Beside her was a face Elena didn't recognize. A young woman in her 30s with dark hair pulled into a tight bun, wearing a conservative suit and carrying a tablet. Everyone, this is Dr. Jennifer Morrison. Mrs. Chin announced she's a researcher from Stamford University. The Whites have agreed to participate in a study about household management, and Dr. Morrison will be conducting interviews with staff members over the next few weeks. Elena felt the blood drain from her face. This was the researcher. This was the study Theodore had been dismissing just hours ago as meaningless theater. Dr. Morrison smiled warmly at the assembled group. Thank you all for your time. I want to assure you that all interviews will be completely confidential. Your names will never be used in any published material. This research is about understanding the realities of domestic work in contemporary America and your voices are essential to that understanding. The irony was so sharp it cut. Here was Elena who had conducted this exact type of research who had written the dissertation that had probably influenced this very study. Now on the other side of the interview table, she was no longer the researcher but the subject. No longer the one analyzing power structures but the one trapped within them. Participation is voluntary, Dr. Morrison continued. But I hope you'll all consider sharing your experiences. Change happens when people are willing to speak truth to power. Elena almost laughed out loud at that phrase, speak truth to power. She had built her entire academic career on that principle. She had believed in it with every fiber of her being. And where had it gotten her? wearing a housekeeper's uniform while someone else did the research she should have been doing. After the meeting, Dr. Morrison approached several staff members individually, scheduling interview times. When she reached Elena, her smile was friendly and professional. Would you be willing to participate? I'm particularly interested in speaking with staff members who have worked in multiple households. It helps me understand patterns across different environments. Elena wanted to refuse. The thought of sitting through an interview, of carefully censoring her words, and performing ignorance about concepts she had spent years studying was almost unbearable. But refusing would draw attention, would mark her as uncooperative or difficult. Sure, I can do that. Wonderful. How about Thursday afternoon? I'll coordinate with Mrs. Chin to make sure it doesn't interfere with your work. Thursday, two days away. Two days to prepare herself for the performance of a lifetime to become the perfect research subject for someone studying the very dynamics she herself had spent years analyzing. That evening, Elena returned to her apartment and did something she hadn't done in months. She opened a bottle of wine, poured herself a generous glass, and pulled out her dissertation. She read through her methodology section, her interview protocols, her analytical framework. She had been so careful, so ethical in her approach to research. She had worked hard to ensure her subjects felt comfortable and safe sharing their experiences. Now she would be on the receiving end of those carefully crafted questions. And she would have to lie. She would have to pretend to be less educated, less aware, less capable of critical analysis than she actually was. Because if Dr. Morrison knew the truth, if anyone in that house knew the truth, everything would fall apart. The woman with a PhD couldn't be trusted to clean bathrooms. The scholar of labor exploitation couldn't be allowed to remain working in a situation she was qualified to critique. her education, her expertise, her entire identity would become a threat rather than an asset. She thought about Theodore's dismissive comments about the research, about Viven's casual certainty that nothing would ever change. They were right in a way. Studies came out all the time. Reports were published. Articles were written. And yet, the fundamental structures remained unchanged because the people with power to change them had no incentive to do so. But they were also wrong. Change did happen, just not in the ways that academic research imagined. Change happened when individuals refused to remain invisible. When they claimed their own narratives and demanded to be seen. The question was whether Elena had the courage to be that person, whether she was willing to risk everything for a truth that might not matter to anyone but herself. She refilled her wine glass and stared at the title page of her dissertation. Dr. Elena Vasquez. That name felt like it belonged to someone else now, someone from a different life, but it was still her name. The work was still her work. And the truths she had documented were still true. Perhaps more true now that she lived them every day. Thursday came too quickly. Elena arrived at work with her stomach and knots, her mind running through possible scenarios and responses. She had rehearsed her performance carefully, deciding how much truth she could safely reveal, how much of herself she could show without revealing too much. Dr. Morrison had set up in a small office adjacent to the library, a space normally used for household administration. When Elena knocked on the open door, the researcher looked up with a welcoming smile. Elena, thank you so much for making time for this. Please have a seat. Can I get you anything? Water. Coffee. Water would be nice. Thank you. The room was arranged to feel less intimidating. Chairs positioned at angles rather than across a desk. A small table with the recorder placed unobtrusively to the side. Elena recognized the setup from her own research days. Create comfort. Establish trust. Make the subject feel heard rather than interrogated. So before we begin, I want to reiterate that everything you share here is confidential. Your name won't be used, and I'll make sure nothing you say can be traced back to you. This isn't about getting anyone in trouble. It's about understanding the realities of domestic work from the perspective of the people who do it." Elena nodded, her hands folded carefully in her lap. She was acutely aware of every gesture, every expression, maintaining the persona of a simple housekeeper who was perhaps a bit nervous about being interviewed. Let's start with some basic background. How long have you been working in domestic service? About 3 years now. Since I moved back to California. And before that, what did you do? This was the moment. Elena could deflect, could claim she had just done odd jobs or worked in retail. That would be the safe answer. But something in her rebelled against making herself smaller than she was. I took care of my mother. She was sick. It wasn't a lie. It just wasn't the complete truth. That must have been difficult. And after she passed, you entered domestic work. Yes. I needed steady income and this job became available. How did you find it? Through an agency. Word of mouth. A friend knew someone who knew someone. The interview continued with questions about daily tasks, working conditions, relationships with employers and other staff. Dr. Morrison was skilled at her job, asking open-ended questions, offering sympathetic responses, creating space for Elena to share more than she intended. "Do you feel respected in your work?" Dr. Morrison asked." Elena paused, considering her answer carefully. "The Whites are fair employers. They pay on time. They don't ask anything unreasonable. That's not quite what I asked. Do you feel respected as a person?" not just as an employee. The directness of the question surprised Elena. This researcher was better than she expected, cutting through polite evasions to get at deeper truths. Respect is complicated in this kind of work, Elena said slowly. I think people respect the work we do because they need it done. But respecting the worker as a full human being, that's different. Can you give me an example? Elena thought about Theodore catching her with his book, about the contempt in his voice when he told her to stay curious about her work, not his. She thought about Viven's casual dismissal of research findings because nothing ever changes. She thought about years of being treated as if her thoughts and feelings existed only in relation to other people's comfort. We're expected to be invisible, she said finally. Not literally, but in terms of our inner lives. No one wants to know what we think or feel beyond whether we're happy with our wages and working conditions. We're supposed to exist only in the context of service. Dr. Morrison leaned forward slightly, her expression intent. That's a very articulate observation. Have you thought about these dynamics much? Warning bells went off in Elena's mind. She had said too much, had let her analytical training show through the cracks in her performance. She needed to pull back to become simpler, less threatening. I guess I just noticed things, she said with a self-deprecating smile. Too much time alone with my thoughts while I clean. The moment passed. Dr. Morrison made a note on her tablet and moved on to other questions, but Elena felt the slip like a near miss accident, a reminder of how carefully she needed to guard herself. The interview lasted an hour. When it was over, Elena returned to her work with her hands shaking slightly. She had survived the encounter, had managed to share just enough truth to be helpful to the research without revealing her own background. But the experience had left her feeling raw and exposed in ways she hadn't anticipated. That afternoon, while cleaning one of the upstairs bathrooms, Elena overheard another conversation that would change everything. The door to Theodore's home office was slightly a jar and voices carried into the hallway where she was working. I need you to run a background check on all the household staff. Theodore's voice was clipped. All business. All of them? Sir, that's unusual. Is there a problem? This was Davidson, Theodore's personal assistant. Just being thorough. This research project has me thinking about security. I want to know exactly who we have working in this house. Elena's blood went cold. Background checks. They would find everything. Her degrees, her dissertation, her entire academic history. There would be questions about why she hadn't disclosed her education, suspicions about her motives for working under false pretenses. She had to think, had to figure out a way to handle this before it exploded. But even as her mind raced through possibilities, she knew the truth. There was no good way out. The life she had carefully constructed, the invisibility she had maintained, it was all about to shatter. And perhaps most terrifying of all, a small part of her felt relieved. Elena barely slept that night. She lay in her small bed, staring at the ceiling, watching shadows from passing cars sweep across the cracked plaster. The background check hung over her like a guillotine, and she knew it was only a matter of time before the blade fell. She had perhaps a week, maybe two if she was lucky, before Theodore's assistant compiled the reports and delivered them to his desk. The question that kept her awake was not whether to run, but whether to stay and fight. Running meant abandoning the life she had built here, modest as it was. It meant starting over somewhere else, hiding her credentials again, living in fear of discovery. But staying meant confronting the inevitable exposure, facing Theodore's anger and the humiliation that would follow when everyone learned she had been deceiving them. Except it wasn't really deception, was it? She had never lied about her education. No one had ever asked. The application for the housekeeping position hadn't required disclosure of advanced degrees. She had simply allowed people to make assumptions based on her uniform and her willingness to clean toilets. If that made her a liar, then what did it make them for never considering that the woman scrubbing their floors might have a mind worth knowing? By dawn, Elena had made her decision.
