But both of the areas, all of this&nbsp; area, is continental. Therefore,&nbsp;&nbsp; it is landlocked, not influenced&nbsp; dramatically by any seas or oceans.&nbsp;&nbsp; You will still get the Gulf Stream effect,&nbsp; though, happening across most of France.&nbsp;&nbsp; Continentality is quite extreme here. That means&nbsp; differences between seasons, so summers tend to&nbsp;&nbsp; be very hot, winters tend to be very cold,&nbsp; and you will find quite a bit of snow. There&nbsp;&nbsp; are also quite big diurnal differences here, so&nbsp; these are the differences between night and day. In a summer month, you’ll easily get around&nbsp; 30 degrees Celsius in the daytime, and then&nbsp;&nbsp; at night-time dropping down to 12 or 13 degrees&nbsp; Celsius. So quite big swings here. This means that&nbsp;&nbsp; the grapes can refresh themselves from the heat of&nbsp; the summer day and get ready for the next day by&nbsp;&nbsp; refreshing at night. That’s really important for&nbsp; maintaining quite high acidities across the board. So Chablis is at the top of the region there,&nbsp; the coolest area, exclusively for Chardonnay,&nbsp;&nbsp; and some of those real linear, lean&nbsp; styles, and very minerally in that area. As we head south, between Dijon and Beaune,&nbsp; the cities of Dijon and Beaune, we have&nbsp;&nbsp; the Côte d’Or. So let’s just put an arrow in here. This whole area, the reddish and the&nbsp; purple area, is called the Côte d’Or.&nbsp;&nbsp; I don’t have any accents on my laptop here,&nbsp; so Côte d’Or for the whole area, split into&nbsp;&nbsp; two zones. The one that’s identified on the right&nbsp; is the Côte de Nuits. The word côte means slope,&nbsp;&nbsp; so this is the slope of Nuits. Nuits is a town&nbsp; situated about two-thirds of the way down that red&nbsp;&nbsp; area, two-thirds of the way down from Dijon.&nbsp; It is famous for Pinot Noir in this area. Below that, we have the Côte de Beaune, so&nbsp; the slopes of Beaune. Beaune is the main city&nbsp;&nbsp; here in this purpley area, the cultural centre&nbsp; for Burgundy as well. It’s a beautiful historical&nbsp;&nbsp; town, a lovely old town in the centre,&nbsp; and of course that Hospices de Beaune,&nbsp;&nbsp; that first picture, is found here as&nbsp; well. So that is the Côte de Beaune. Then we have a slightly larger expanse to the&nbsp; south of that, to the west of Chalon-sur-Saône,&nbsp;&nbsp; and this is the Côte Chalonnaise, so the slopes of&nbsp;&nbsp; Chalon. This is kind of an overspill area&nbsp; from the Côte de Beaune and the Côte d’Or,&nbsp;&nbsp; and there are some good examples of both&nbsp; Chardonnay and Pinot Noir found here. The Côte de Beaune is mainly Chardonnay.&nbsp; It’s about half and half, but it is mainly&nbsp;&nbsp; Chardonnay in that area. Finally at the bottom, which is&nbsp; predominantly Chardonnay, is the Mâconnais,&nbsp;&nbsp; just north of the city of Mâcon and just on&nbsp; the border of Beaujolais. This is a large,&nbsp;&nbsp; large expanse of some more simple&nbsp; wines, for the most part, in this area. I just wanted to give you,&nbsp; therefore, an idea of the location.&nbsp;&nbsp; As you move down that list from Chablis to&nbsp; Mâconnais, you get from cool to moderate.&nbsp;&nbsp; This means there is more ripening in&nbsp; the grape varieties as you head south.&nbsp;&nbsp; There is a marked difference between Chardonnay in&nbsp; Chablis and Chardonnay in Mâconnais, for instance. You’ll often find in Chablis Chardonnay&nbsp; very linear, green apple — by green apple,&nbsp;&nbsp; I mean Granny Smith green apple — very tart, and&nbsp; things like minerally, lemony characteristics.&nbsp;&nbsp; In the Mâconnais, because of the warmth here&nbsp; and the better ripening, you’ll tend to find&nbsp;&nbsp; slightly more red apple or sweeter apple notes,&nbsp; and things like a little bit of stone fruit,&nbsp;&nbsp; like peach as well, with some&nbsp; good acidities behind those.&nbsp;&nbsp; So there is a diversity within this region,&nbsp; which is fascinating, of course, in one region. That’s really the macroclimate of the area,&nbsp; with a few little breakdowns there between&nbsp;&nbsp; the north and the south. It is important&nbsp; to talk about weather hazards here, though,&nbsp;&nbsp; because Burgundy is subject to quite&nbsp; a lot of weather hazards. This means&nbsp;&nbsp; we have some issues here, and it’s actually quite&nbsp; difficult to cultivate the vine in this region. First and foremost: rainfall. Most of France, and&nbsp; certainly most of the west, but also the north&nbsp;&nbsp; and parts of the centre of France, are&nbsp; affected by the very wet and warm Gulf Stream,&nbsp;&nbsp; which is pulled across the Atlantic,&nbsp; starting in the Central Americas,&nbsp;&nbsp; pulled across the Atlantic, and then brings a&nbsp; lot of wet and warm weather to western Europe. This can affect this area. A lot of it falls, of&nbsp; course, on western France and northern France, but&nbsp;&nbsp; it can be pulled across towards Burgundy. So&nbsp; we will have rainfall in this area. It’s not&nbsp;&nbsp; as much as, say, something like Bordeaux, but&nbsp; there is still rainfall throughout the year. The two specific times they would like&nbsp; you to understand that it’s the most&nbsp;&nbsp; impactful are flowering, so that is&nbsp; going to be around June-ish time.&nbsp;&nbsp; If there are these June showers,&nbsp; June rainfall, that will disrupt&nbsp;&nbsp; flowering. It may actually create very&nbsp; poor flowering and lower the fruit set,&nbsp;&nbsp; so how much fruit is produced from the flowering.&nbsp; This can drastically reduce yields at this point. Then, fast-forwarding towards the end of the&nbsp; year and harvest time, so September/October,&nbsp;&nbsp; you will find normally around September&nbsp; that there can be rainfall around harvest&nbsp;&nbsp; as well. Both the varieties, Chardonnay&nbsp; and Pinot Noir, are subject to this,&nbsp;&nbsp; and they do suffer with rot, specifically&nbsp; Pinot Noir. If this happens, of course,&nbsp;&nbsp; a lot of fruit will have to be eliminated&nbsp; because it can cause off-flavours in the&nbsp;&nbsp; wine. That will drastically reduce yields.&nbsp; So harvest rainfall can be a big issue. Then we have spring frosts. Both Chardonnay and&nbsp; Pinot Noir, which are related grape varieties,&nbsp;&nbsp; are sadly early-budding grape varieties. They&nbsp; are both subject to spring frost because they run&nbsp;&nbsp; the gauntlet, the risk, more of being affected by&nbsp; spring frost because of their early budding cycle.&nbsp;&nbsp; These spring frosts, which can occur in April and&nbsp; May, can of course cause a lot of issues within&nbsp;&nbsp; the region. They can dramatically&nbsp; reduce the yields, big time. We actually go into a lot more&nbsp; detail in the Chablis section about&nbsp;&nbsp; ways to combat spring frosts, but&nbsp; it’s clearly in your textbooks about&nbsp;&nbsp; the major methods. There are better sites of&nbsp; vineyards that are not so prone to frosts,&nbsp;&nbsp; and they’re on slopes. It’s one of&nbsp; the important factors of slopes. But&nbsp;&nbsp; it can be an issue here: spring frost, and&nbsp; that can dramatically reduce the yields. Then, because of the&nbsp; continentality of this region,&nbsp;&nbsp; there can be some localised&nbsp; and quite intensive hailstorms.&nbsp;&nbsp; Hail is big bits of ice which are pelted out of&nbsp; the sky down to the ground at terminal velocity,&nbsp;&nbsp; and can damage and destroy many things, including&nbsp; cars and houses and glass panes, and you name it.&nbsp;&nbsp; Vineyards are no exception. They will be affected&nbsp; by hailstorms and they can be damaged, they can be&nbsp;&nbsp; destroyed throughout summer. It depends on when&nbsp; exactly, but they can shred leaves, they can&nbsp;&nbsp; damage flowering, they can damage the grapes&nbsp; themselves, leading to splitting and rotting. Often, if it’s such a powerful hailstorm, it&nbsp; will in fact wipe out a whole local area for&nbsp;&nbsp; vineyards and other plants. So hailstorms can be&nbsp; a bit of an issue. One way that this is combated&nbsp;&nbsp; against is that grapes are actually sourced from&nbsp; all over Burgundy for certain wines. Maybe your&nbsp;&nbsp; grapes are affected in one village, but you do&nbsp; have grapes from other villages that you purchase&nbsp;&nbsp; or that you own. Therefore, it won’t ruin the&nbsp; whole entire crop of your production, your labels. Now we’re going to talk a little bit about&nbsp; the layout of the land and a little bit about&nbsp;&nbsp; geology. They don’t go into too much detail&nbsp; about that for the Level 3 certificate,&nbsp;&nbsp; but in Burgundy, because it is&nbsp; generally a cool area, cool to moderate,&nbsp;&nbsp; the importance of slope cannot be&nbsp; underestimated. It’s very important. What we have, of course, is many areas named after&nbsp; slopes. You have the Côte d’Or, Côte de Beaune,&nbsp;&nbsp; Côte Chalonnaise, etc. That means, of course, they&nbsp; are dominated by some form of slopes. The slopes&nbsp;&nbsp; all lie on the western side and the vineyards are&nbsp; on the east, so facing east. This is remarkably&nbsp;&nbsp; important because that means they are protected&nbsp; from that Gulf Stream, from that westerly wind. Let’s scribble that in. What you’ll have&nbsp; coming across here is the westerly wind,&nbsp;&nbsp; and that westerly wind is buffered&nbsp; and mitigated by the hills and the&nbsp;&nbsp; forests that are on the hills.&nbsp; Therefore, the slopes that lie&nbsp;&nbsp; on the eastern side remain quite protected from&nbsp; that westerly wind, so that’s quite important. Also, the slope offers a couple of things. It&nbsp; offers better drainage. So when you have rainfall&nbsp;&nbsp; — let’s draw in a little bit of rainfall here as&nbsp; well — when we have rainfall coming down here,&nbsp;&nbsp; at the bottom of the slope, where the&nbsp; village and the regional AOCs are,&nbsp;&nbsp; that can actually become quite inundated&nbsp; with water because it doesn’t drain. It stays&nbsp;&nbsp; in the topsoil, where your root systems are. In the premier crus, it will tend to actually&nbsp; run down as surface runoff, so that runs down&nbsp;&nbsp; the vineyard. Grand crus drain very well, but&nbsp; some water is retained for what the vine needs,&nbsp;&nbsp; so it has the perfect balance in&nbsp; that middle area. Hence why we find&nbsp;&nbsp; our very best wines, like grand cru and&nbsp; premier cru, in the middle of the slope area. The mid-slope is remarkably important for that&nbsp;&nbsp; good drainage. The slopes are good for&nbsp; protection against the westerly wind,&nbsp;&nbsp; and the third reason would be to do with aspect,&nbsp; which I believe is what we’ll go through next. Yes, so aspect is the next reason of slopes.&nbsp;&nbsp; What is the importance of aspect?&nbsp; Aspect is the direction of our slope,&nbsp;&nbsp; and therefore which way it faces. Here I’ve&nbsp; given you some points of the compass. We’ve&nbsp;&nbsp; gone from west on this side through south-west,&nbsp; south, south-east and east. This is just to give&nbsp;&nbsp; you an idea, and specifically with Pinot Noir,&nbsp; in fact, about the direction of your slope. Here in Burgundy, most slopes come from&nbsp; south to east, so to this area: south,&nbsp;&nbsp; south-east and east-facing slopes. That’s because,&nbsp;&nbsp; remember, they are on the eastern side of the&nbsp; hills and are protected from the westerly wind. If you grow Pinot Noir on an east-facing vineyard&nbsp; site on a slope, this will get the sunshine more&nbsp;&nbsp; in the morning, which is very gentle sunshine. As&nbsp; of course the earth is spinning around the sun,&nbsp;&nbsp; the sun moves into its zenith in the&nbsp; south and then towards the western part.&nbsp;&nbsp; Easterly-facing slopes will get more&nbsp; morning sun, less afternoon sun. That means, because the morning sun is much&nbsp; cooler and much gentler as solar radiation,&nbsp;&nbsp; the grapes will ripen, but more&nbsp; elegantly, more delicately. So&nbsp;&nbsp; Pinot Noir on east-facing slopes will often be a&nbsp; little bit more “feminine”, they say, or elegant&nbsp;&nbsp; and not so concentrated, but they&nbsp; can have beautiful acidities. A south-east-facing slope will have a little bit&nbsp; better ripening because, of course, it is now more&nbsp;&nbsp; facing south, and this is in Burgundy, so this is&nbsp; in the northern hemisphere. That south-easterly&nbsp;&nbsp; aspect means that there’s a bit more of the&nbsp; sunshine around midday and towards midday, so&nbsp;&nbsp; that means that’ll be a bit more ripened. In fact,&nbsp; a lot of vineyards in Burgundy face south-east. Then south-facing: we have&nbsp; some in Burgundy facing south,&nbsp;&nbsp; and of course this is going to&nbsp; get much better sunlight around&nbsp;&nbsp; midday and certainly around lunchtime, when&nbsp; the sun is a bit more powerful, of course,&nbsp;&nbsp; and there’s more heat and more ripening and&nbsp; solar radiation. So these will be more intense. If you were to then grow Pinot Noir on&nbsp; south-west-facing, or even west-facing,&nbsp;&nbsp; more south-west, which bits are in the Côte&nbsp; d’Or, like Santenay, they will have that very&nbsp;&nbsp; aggressive afternoon sunshine, which tends to&nbsp; really ripen the grapes. In fact, some parts&nbsp;&nbsp; of Burgundy which are found on south-west-facing&nbsp; slopes, critics often label these when they taste&nbsp;&nbsp; them in big tastings as like a New World style.&nbsp; That reminds them of more ripened, sometimes&nbsp;&nbsp; dried fruits, and much more concentration, maybe&nbsp; less balance, but certainly more concentration. So aspect, certainly with elegant&nbsp; grapes and delicate grapes like&nbsp;&nbsp; Pinot Noir, can have a huge impact&nbsp; in terms of the actual style.&nbsp;&nbsp; Once again, most of our slopes here face sort&nbsp; of east to south in our vineyards of Burgundy. So the key grape varieties now. Because&nbsp; we will go through Chardonnay in great&nbsp;&nbsp; detail when we look at the Côte de Beaune&nbsp; and other regions like the Mâconnais,&nbsp;&nbsp; I’m just actually mentioning what&nbsp; Chardonnay is good for here in&nbsp;&nbsp; terms of its production. It is the key&nbsp; grape variety, about 50% of production,&nbsp;&nbsp; and that’s really because of Chardonnay’s&nbsp; popularity in the last 30 or 40 years or so. So half of production here is&nbsp; made, of course, in a linear,&nbsp;&nbsp; light style, which is quite&nbsp; minerally up in places like&nbsp;&nbsp; Chablis, and can be completely complex, rich,&nbsp; malolactic, buttery, oaky in certain other&nbsp;&nbsp; areas and village commune zones as well. So&nbsp; Chardonnay is a very, very key grape variety. About a third of production is Pinot Noir, and&nbsp; Pinot Noir is found mainly in the Côte de Nuits&nbsp;&nbsp; for us, but there is some&nbsp; in both the Côte de Beaune&nbsp;&nbsp; and the Côte Chalonnaise. Pinot Noir, as I&nbsp; mentioned, is really site-specific. Aspect,&nbsp;&nbsp; slope, all of those have a dramatic part&nbsp; to play in the overall style of the wine. Both of these grape varieties are old grape&nbsp; varieties. We have a lot of history behind them&nbsp;&nbsp; from this region. They have also, therefore,&nbsp; adapted lots of different clones of these&nbsp;&nbsp; varieties. Along with the site, the vineyard&nbsp; itself, things like aspect and so on, the clonal&nbsp;&nbsp; influences are quite specific here as&nbsp; well. Different clones have different&nbsp;&nbsp; adaptable characteristics. Maybe they are&nbsp; better at water absorption, maybe they are&nbsp;&nbsp; better in more drought-like conditions, maybe&nbsp; they’re better on different aspects, and so on.&nbsp;&nbsp; So there are a lot of clonal types of&nbsp; both of these available in Burgundy. We have Aligoté. Aligoté is a grape variety&nbsp; which is a cousin of Chardonnay and Pinot Noir,&nbsp;&nbsp; but unfortunately it’s kind of always been the&nbsp; ugly duckling, and it’s always been left behind,&nbsp;&nbsp; bless it. Because it is the ugly&nbsp; duckling, I have a soft spot for it. So what happens with this? Well, Aligoté has had&nbsp; to contend with Chardonnay for over a thousand&nbsp;&nbsp; years. It’s lost that battle, so Chardonnay&nbsp; is found in our most favourable areas where&nbsp;&nbsp; Aligoté isn’t. Aligoté is normally on flatter&nbsp; land. It produces more volume and often more&nbsp;&nbsp; neutral wines with high acidities. But there are&nbsp; some good areas, and certainly some producers are&nbsp;&nbsp; focusing on this grape variety. If you do find it&nbsp; on favourable areas with good aspect and slope,&nbsp;&nbsp; Aligoté, and good clones of Aligoté, can produce&nbsp; really wonderfully aromatic and textured wines.&nbsp;&nbsp; So it’s a bit of a Jekyll and Hyde&nbsp; grape variety, due to the fact&nbsp;&nbsp; really of its ambitious cousin Chardonnay,&nbsp; which has kind of over-dominated it for so long. Then we have Gamay, of course. We have Gamay.&nbsp; Gamay is a grape variety which has been populating&nbsp;&nbsp; most of France, and certainly central parts&nbsp; of France, for many years. It’s a grape that&nbsp;&nbsp; the aristocracy of old have hated, and it’s a&nbsp; grape of the people. Gamay yields very well,&nbsp;&nbsp; often four times the amount of Pinot Noir, and&nbsp; can also be left unchecked and left alone, and it&nbsp;&nbsp; will still really look after itself. It’s a very&nbsp; solid grape variety, Gamay. A very solid vine. Here in Burgundy, because of course Gamay&nbsp; we’ll talk about in Beaujolais more,&nbsp;&nbsp; Gamay is considered just an early-drinking&nbsp; style. It is found in many blends,&nbsp;&nbsp; and yes, in more simple Burgundian wines you can&nbsp; find a bit of Gamay, but light, gentle stuff. Okay, so now we’re going to go through the&nbsp; hierarchy, give you some examples of those&nbsp;&nbsp; hierarchy levels, and then we’ll go through some&nbsp; questions, some short written-answer questions. Here is your triangle of the hierarchy&nbsp; in Burgundy. At the bottom of the ladder,&nbsp;&nbsp; according to what you need&nbsp; to know for your textbooks,&nbsp;&nbsp; you need to know that there are some regional&nbsp; appellations in Burgundy, such as Bourgogne Rouge&nbsp;&nbsp; and Bourgogne Blanc. Both of these are regional&nbsp; wines. The grapes will be sourced from all over&nbsp;&nbsp; Burgundy, and they will generally be an&nbsp; introduction style, or a simpler style,&nbsp;&nbsp; of both red and white. So Bourgogne Rouge and&nbsp; Bourgogne Blanc will normally be dominated by&nbsp;&nbsp; Pinot Noir and Chardonnay respectively,&nbsp; and often found on flatter lands,&nbsp;&nbsp; places which will have poorer drainage,&nbsp; more volume, less concentration, and so on. There are some regional areas as well.&nbsp; So the regional zones are broken into&nbsp;&nbsp; what used to be called districts, now&nbsp; sub-regional zones. These are things like&nbsp;&nbsp; Bourgogne Hautes-Côtes de Nuits, so Burgundy upper&nbsp; slopes of Nuits, Bourgogne Hautes-Côtes de Beaune,&nbsp;&nbsp; so Burgundy upper slopes of Beaune,&nbsp; Mâcon and Mâcon-Villages, for instance.&nbsp;&nbsp; These are more sub-regional. There are&nbsp; more inherent terroir aspects to them. What we mean by that is terroir concepts, really.&nbsp; They have specific locations. There’s a bit more&nbsp;&nbsp; of a microclimate about these zones. For example,&nbsp; the Bourgogne Hautes-Côtes de Nuits is actually on&nbsp;&nbsp; the top slopes, on the upper slopes in the hills&nbsp; behind the very famous south-east-facing slope&nbsp;&nbsp; of the Côte de Nuits. This is an area, therefore,&nbsp; that’s a bit more affected by the elements.&nbsp;&nbsp; It’s a bit more sporadic up there, and it&nbsp; can be, you know, wines that have a bit&nbsp;&nbsp; more concentration than a regional,&nbsp; but less than a village level. Above that, these regions and sub-regions are&nbsp; populated with villages. A commune is a village.&nbsp;&nbsp; So if the grapes are exclusively sourced from that&nbsp; village, it can bear the name of that village on&nbsp;&nbsp; the label. Examples of this include Chablis, of&nbsp; course; Gevrey-Chambertin in the Côte de Nuits;&nbsp;&nbsp; Meursault in the Côte de Beaune; Mercurey, which&nbsp; is in the Côte Chalonnaise; Pouilly-Fuissé,&nbsp;&nbsp; which is in the Mâconnais; and then Beaune,&nbsp; which is in the Côte de Beaune, of course. They are very well respected. They’ve been known&nbsp; over centuries and centuries, so of course they&nbsp;&nbsp; hold a lot of weight. A lot of people will tend to&nbsp; produce village-level commune wines if they can. Within each of those villages will be sites&nbsp; of very good importance, and they are called&nbsp;&nbsp; premier cru. So you’ll have on the label the&nbsp; name of the village, the commune, and the premier&nbsp;&nbsp; cru vineyard, the named vineyard. The example&nbsp; they’re giving there is Beaune Grèves. Beaune&nbsp;&nbsp; is our village; Grèves is the vineyard. These&nbsp; are going to be, of course, on some of the slope&nbsp;&nbsp; areas, facing south-easterly, east and south, with&nbsp; good drainage, often around the grand cru areas. Then the top one, of course, is grand cru. These&nbsp; are the highest-quality vineyard sites, the best&nbsp;&nbsp; areas, as we’ve just gone through, within a&nbsp; village, within a commune. So you’ll have Le&nbsp;&nbsp; Montrachet, which of course is in the Montrachet&nbsp; villages; Le Corton, which is within Aloxe-Corton,&nbsp;&nbsp; on the Corton hill; and the list will go on&nbsp; for these. These are your most expensive,&nbsp;&nbsp; premium and highest-quality-level wines. They are&nbsp; on the best sites, best slopes, best drainage,&nbsp;&nbsp; and often they’ll have a hell&nbsp; of a lot of history behind them,&nbsp;&nbsp; having been produced here for thousands&nbsp; of years. So that is your grand cru level. To put it into labelling for you, just&nbsp; so you’ve got some clear labels here,&nbsp;&nbsp; you have a Bourgogne Pinot Noir label from&nbsp; Château de Dracy, and that is from Albert Bichot.&nbsp;&nbsp; This is Bourgogne Pinot Noir, so it just means&nbsp; a Burgundy Pinot Noir. That is a regional label. Then you have a sub-regional&nbsp; label, which is Albert Bichot&nbsp;&nbsp; again. This is Côte de Nuits, so this&nbsp; is a sub-region as a part of Burgundy.&nbsp;&nbsp; So that’s around the Côte de Nuits, but&nbsp; all of the village areas around that. Then you have the village. So Albert Bichot,&nbsp; Vosne-Romanée is the name of the village.&nbsp;&nbsp; Within that village, you can have&nbsp; premier cru sites, of course. This is&nbsp;&nbsp; the named village and then&nbsp; premier cru: Les Malconsorts.&nbsp;&nbsp; That is the premier cru name of&nbsp; that vineyard. So you have the&nbsp;&nbsp; village level, Vosne-Romanée, and then you&nbsp; have the named vineyard, Les Malconsorts. Then some of these villages, but not all,&nbsp; have grand cru, and in Vosne-Romanée there&nbsp;&nbsp; is Richebourg. It says grand cru on the&nbsp; label. With a grand cru, it will not put&nbsp;&nbsp; the name of the village. It just leads with the&nbsp; name of the grand cru. So this says Richebourg,&nbsp;&nbsp; which is a very famous,&nbsp; very famous one, of course. That is going from more acceptable quality,&nbsp;&nbsp; from regional, to grand cru, which of course&nbsp; is exceptionally high premium quality. Okay, so just to wrap up the next few minutes,&nbsp;&nbsp; some questions associated&nbsp; with what we just learned.&nbsp;&nbsp; This is our working written question. Name three weather hazards and their&nbsp; effect on the vines in Burgundy. So&nbsp;&nbsp; how do these hazards affect the vines in Burgundy? First of all: a reduction&nbsp; of yield. This is an effect&nbsp;&nbsp; with summer rainfall that can disrupt&nbsp; flowering, and harvest rainfall that can&nbsp;&nbsp; cause rot. That is enough to get you two marks.&nbsp; In fact, that’s enough to really get you three,&nbsp;&nbsp; but it’s actually just two marks per hazard&nbsp; here. So that’s your first one, which we went&nbsp;&nbsp; through earlier. Pinot Noir is specifically&nbsp; susceptible to those harvest rainfalls. Hazard two: spring frosts can occur up to May.&nbsp; They reduce yields by damaging or destroying&nbsp;&nbsp; the buds. Some of this wording won’t be directly&nbsp; found in the Burgundy section of your text. What&nbsp;&nbsp; you are required to do, and how you should link&nbsp; to understand, is link something you’ve learned&nbsp;&nbsp; earlier on in the book about the viticultural&nbsp; aspects, general viticultural aspects. It mentions in the book that spring&nbsp; frosts here can occur up to May,&nbsp;&nbsp; but it doesn’t say anything else. You are&nbsp; expected to link it to earlier in your textbook,&nbsp;&nbsp; which says they reduce yields by damaging&nbsp; and destroying buds, the general concept. Hazard three is the hailstorm.&nbsp; Summer localised hailstorms&nbsp;&nbsp; can damage or destroy the vines, leading&nbsp; to a reduction in yield. The same again:&nbsp;&nbsp; the only thing that’s mentioned in your&nbsp; Burgundy section is summer hailstorms,&nbsp;&nbsp; so you need to then add what you would learn&nbsp; earlier on in the course to that. It’s your&nbsp;&nbsp; understanding of the concept. That will be six&nbsp; marks, two marks per hazard in that instance. Next up is: describe two reasons why Pinot Noir is&nbsp; particularly a challenge to grow here in Burgundy. Immediately, we know it’s a cool or&nbsp; moderate area. That is not a problem with&nbsp;&nbsp; Pinot Noir. Pinot actually prefers that. It is an&nbsp; early ripener, so please don’t put down anything&nbsp;&nbsp; around the fact that Pinot&nbsp; Noir is an early ripener,&nbsp;&nbsp; because that’s actually a benefit.&nbsp; We’re looking at problems. So Pinot Noir is early budding. That is a problem&nbsp; because there is a greater risk of spring frosts,&nbsp;&nbsp; which of course will occur&nbsp; somewhere like April or May.&nbsp;&nbsp; Pinot Noir therefore runs the risk&nbsp; of being affected by spring frost. Reason two is Pinot Noir, really due to its&nbsp; thin skins, is quite susceptible to rot,&nbsp;&nbsp; and yields can be dramatically affected by&nbsp; those harvest rainfalls. Pinot Noir is quite&nbsp;&nbsp; subject to that. So there will be&nbsp; two marks available per reason there. Describe the likely viticultural conditions of a&nbsp; grand cru or premier cru vineyard. Now, in your&nbsp;&nbsp; text, they kind of group these two together.&nbsp; Grand cru is the best, but premier cru can&nbsp;&nbsp; sometimes be better, but they’re normally&nbsp; just below in quality, but near in quality.&nbsp;&nbsp; So we need to understand what you would&nbsp; expect in the vineyard with these sites. First of all, they are likely to be found&nbsp; on mid-slopes where there is great drainage&nbsp;&nbsp; and less frost risk because&nbsp; there’s good aeration on a slope.&nbsp;&nbsp; I’ve highlighted three things there because that&nbsp; will get you three marks in that one sentence:&nbsp;&nbsp; mid-slopes, good drainage and less frost risk. The frost risk is mentioned in your Burgundy&nbsp; part. The drainage is something you should&nbsp;&nbsp; just understand about slope, the effect&nbsp; of a slope, the fact it will drain better&nbsp;&nbsp; in that area. That will be found in&nbsp; the earlier parts of your textbook. The next part is: in addition, the aspect&nbsp; of these slopes will often be south to east,&nbsp;&nbsp; so that’s one mark there, offering&nbsp; protection from westerly winds, that’s&nbsp;&nbsp; one mark, and better but gentle ripening&nbsp; conditions as they are facing the sun,&nbsp;&nbsp; or have good aspect towards the sun.&nbsp; That will get you your sixth mark. There are other things that potentially&nbsp; could be mentioned here, but it’s difficult&nbsp;&nbsp; to generalise. You could say often that&nbsp; the vines are generally older in grand&nbsp;&nbsp; crus and premier crus, but it’s not always&nbsp; the case. - Can the fine wine market maintain its performance? | FT Wealth: Kind: captions Language: en-GB Few would argue that we aren't living in turbulent times. But today I've come to a particularly serene part of southeast England to find out how an evolving fine wine market is responding to an assortment of traditional and not so traditional challenges, ranging from long-term weather patterns to pandemics. It's very strong. The market has moved from being a UK-centric type investment to right now, worldwide. And there are more platforms cycling up all the time. In fact, the Live-ex 100, the leading industry exchange of the secondary market, has risen by around a third over the past two years. They are very, very good at putting out information as to the state of the wine market in general. And they are saying that it's extremely robust. Over the past 10 years, when it comes to luxury investments' performance, wine has trailed only cars and rare whisky, according to the Knight Frank Investment Index. But this global market is facing a global issue that could well affect investment values and prices in the longer term - climate change. The major wine regions for investment are all on the same latitude. So you start off with Napa Valley in California, on the West Coast of the United States, through to Bordeaux, through to Burgundy. And of course, these regions are getting hotter and/or colder. As our climate changes, it could make it harder to produce future vintages of the quality we're used to. Climate change is going to play a part in the wine life. That said, with wine, if there is no more Bordeaux, then the wines that are held with customers are only going to increase in value. Of course, climate change could eventually influence supply the other way by actually opening up new areas for quality wine production. The Champagne region of France, for example, is an area seen as being particularly susceptible to climate change, which is why famed champagne houses, like Pomeroy and Taittinger, are now looking to places they would never previously have considered for production. Big international players have come into the English wine scene to invest in their own vineyards, put their money where their mouth is, and recognise how special the southeast of England is. The effect of climate change will, of course, be gradual. But I have come to the UK's oldest wine merchant, Berry Brothers, founded in 1698, to learn more about how certain aspects of the markets are changing now. Some good ordinary clarets, are they? Yes, my favourite. The company stores around £1bn worth of wine for private clients. There may be a few thousand bottles here, maybe. I actually don't know the exact figure. In Basingstoke we currently have a facility for 9mn bottles. Spending on fine wines actually increased throughout the recent pandemic. And sales for its en Primeur season, which allows collectors to buy the most recent vintage before it is bottled, were up over 40 per cent in 2021 compared with 2019. As sales grow, and with greater volumes being traded across the world, ensuring the origin and care of their wine is as it should be is becoming ever more important for collectors. The bulk of what I do is looking to check that everything that we sell is absolutely as pristine as it should be, and that it's come from the right place so that the provenance is correct, that it's been stored correctly on the way into us. In times of higher inflation, as we're seeing now, physical assets do have the potential to offer solid returns. But wine can entail higher costs than other investments due to factors like shipping and storage. It's seldom a short-term investment, and concerns about counterfeiting are also becoming more prominent. It wasn't really an issue up until about 10 years ago. Now it's something which everyone asks us about. Cases like that of Rudy Kurniawan, who in 2013 was convicted of wine fraud for selling tens of millions of fake wines, have thrown a spotlight on counterfeiting. So how big of a problem is wine fraud nowadays? The more common types of wine fraud is mass produced bottling of perfectly potable table wine with a more expensive label on it. And that's happening probably as we speak. Somewhere in the world, somebody is bottling something which is not what it says on the label. Combating the frauds takes various techniques and technologies, both old and more recent. Can you see the fluorescing lights and the fluorescing label there? Yeah. But there are two tiny little holograms in the shape of a cloverleaf. Their position moves with every vintage. Other verification systems can range from nanochips to tracking numbers and even a system that matches a unique pattern of air bubbles under the cap to an image kept on file. One relatively recent development uses blockchain technology to help gain clarity over a bottle's condition and whereabouts across the supply chain. When you've got a broken supply chain, actually, that's the best time, in my opinion, to commit fraud. So what we're doing is saying, actually, we may make 5,000 or 500, depending on what's going out of a QR code that will be unique to that bottle and that batch. Can you show me how this sort of technology would actually work in practise then? We're going to scan that code as so, and now we're going to go into temperature, humidity, and GPS location of that bottle. Systems like Chainvine won't be for everyone. But in a global survey of wineries last year, blockchain technology ranked second of all technologies they saw as having the highest priority of importance to develop. As for the wine investment market, if it wants to build on strong recent performance, it will have to adapt to the constant changes coming from both inside and outside the industry.

I was the same as you, I never played, knew of the game. I watched how to play and liked it. Went all in (played 5 times in the last week)!

Packing the box with the acrylic tiles is a problem. if you put all the tiles in their bags and get rid of the vineyard storage tray you can stack everything so the bags sit at the top and the lid shuts perfectly. (vineyard boards > playerboards + vineyard shop next to it > castles tray > gameboard + rulebook > goods insert tray>  tile bags)
Ill get some extra trays to sit on the top space for the coins + workers just for better gameplay, but they do fit in tray.

It is that frustration that there is something you want to execute on your board - but your dice roll just didn’t let you achieve it that made me difficult to love this game…
